<th scope="row">Religion</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a><sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></td>

<p><b>Frederick II</b> (26 December 1194 – 13 December 1250; <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Friedrich II.</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <span lang="it" xml:lang="it"><i>Federico II</i></span>) was a <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> and <a href="/wiki/King_of_Sicily" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Sicily">King of Sicily</a> in the Middle Ages, a member of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hohenstaufen" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Hohenstaufen">House of Hohenstaufen</a>. His political and cultural ambitions, based in Sicily and stretching through Italy to Germany, and even to Jerusalem, were enormous. However, his enemies, especially the popes, prevailed, and his dynasty collapsed soon after his death.</p>
<p>Viewing himself as a direct successor to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperors</a> of antiquity,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> he was <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor of the Romans</a> from his papal coronation in 1220 until his death; he was also a claimant to the title of <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Romans" title="King of the Romans">King of the Romans</a> from 1212 and unopposed holder of that monarchy from 1215. As such, he was <a href="/wiki/King_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Germany">King of Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_of_Italy" title="King of Italy">of Italy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/King_of_Burgundy" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Burgundy">of Burgundy</a>. At the age of three, he was crowned <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">King of Sicily</a> as a co-ruler with his mother, <a href="/wiki/Constance_of_Sicily" class="mw-redirect" title="Constance of Sicily">Constance of Hauteville</a>, the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">Roger II of Sicily</a>. His other royal title was <a href="/wiki/King_of_Jerusalem" title="King of Jerusalem">King of Jerusalem</a> by virtue of marriage and his connection with the <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Crusade" title="Sixth Crusade">Sixth Crusade</a>.</p>
<p>He was frequently at war with the papacy, hemmed in between Frederick's lands in northern Italy and his <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a> (the <i>Regno</i>) to the south, and thus he was <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunicated</a> four times and often vilified in pro-papal chronicles of the time and since. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_IX" title="Pope Gregory IX">Pope Gregory IX</a> went so far as to call him an <a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">Antichrist</a>.</p>
<p>Speaking six languages (Latin, Sicilian, German, French, Greek and Arabic<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>), Frederick was an avid patron of science and the arts. He played a major role in promoting literature through the <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_School" title="Sicilian School">Sicilian School</a> of poetry. His Sicilian royal court in <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>, from around 1220 to his death, saw the first use of a literary form of an <a href="/wiki/Italo-Romance" class="mw-redirect" title="Italo-Romance">Italo-Romance</a> language, <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_language" title="Sicilian language">Sicilian</a>. The poetry that emanated from the school had a significant influence on literature and on what was to become the modern <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was also the first king who explicitly outlawed <a href="/wiki/Trial_by_ordeal" title="Trial by ordeal">trials by ordeal</a> as they were considered irrational.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>After his death, his line quickly died out and the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hohenstaufen" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Hohenstaufen">House of Hohenstaufen</a> came to an end.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frederick_II_birth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Frederick_II_birth.jpg/250px-Frederick_II_birth.jpg" width="250" height="328" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Frederick_II_birth.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="393" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frederick_II_birth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The birth of Frederick&#160;II</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Iesi" title="Iesi">Iesi</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Ancona" title="Ancona">Ancona</a>, Italy, Frederick was the son of the emperor <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VI, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry VI</a>. He was known as the <i>puer Apuliae</i> (son of <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Some chronicles say that his mother, the forty-year-old <a href="/wiki/Constance_of_Sicily" class="mw-redirect" title="Constance of Sicily">Constance</a>, gave birth to him in a public square in order to forestall any doubt about his origin. Frederick was baptised in <a href="/wiki/Assisi" title="Assisi">Assisi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1196 at <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_am_Main" class="mw-redirect" title="Frankfurt am Main">Frankfurt am Main</a> the infant Frederick was elected <a href="/wiki/List_of_German_Kings_and_Emperors" class="mw-redirect" title="List of German Kings and Emperors">King of the Germans</a>. His rights in Germany were <a href="/wiki/German_throne_dispute" title="German throne dispute">disputed</a> by Henry's brother <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Swabia" title="Philip of Swabia">Philip of Swabia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otto_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto of Brunswick</a>. At the death of his father in 1197, Frederick was in Italy traveling towards Germany when the bad news reached his guardian, <a href="/wiki/Conrad_I,_Duke_of_Spoleto" title="Conrad I, Duke of Spoleto">Conrad of Spoleto</a>. Frederick was hastily brought back to his mother Constance in Palermo, Sicily, where he was crowned as King on 17 May 1198, now Frederick I of Sicily, at only three years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Constance of Sicily was in her own right queen of Sicily, and she established herself as <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a>. In Frederick's name she dissolved Sicily's ties to Germany and the Empire that had been created by her marriage, sending home his German counsellors and renouncing his claims to the German throne and empire.</p>
<p>Upon Constance's death in 1198, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Pope Innocent III</a> succeeded as Frederick's guardian. Frederick's tutor during this period was <a href="/wiki/Honorius_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Honorius III">Cencio</a>, who would become Pope Honorius III.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> However, Markward of Annweiler, with the support of Henry's brother, <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Swabia" title="Philip of Swabia">Philip of Swabia</a>, reclaimed the regency for himself and soon after invaded the Kingdom of Naples. In 1200, with the help of <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese ships</a>, he landed in Sicily and one year later seized the young Frederick.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> He thus ruled Sicily until 1202, when he was succeeded by another German captain, <a href="/wiki/William_of_Capparone" title="William of Capparone">William of Capparone</a>, who kept Frederick under his control in the royal palace of Palermo until 1206. Frederick was subsequently under tutor <a href="/wiki/Walter_of_Palearia" title="Walter of Palearia">Walter of Palearia</a>, until, in 1208, he was declared of age. His first task was to reassert his power over Sicily and southern Italy, where local barons and adventurers had usurped most of the authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emperor">Emperor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Emperor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table style="background: #FFFFFF; border: 1px solid #8888AA; border-collapse: collapse; float: left; font-size: 95%; margin: 8px 8px 0px 0px; padding: none; text-align: left; vertical-align: top; width: 200px;" cellpadding="4" cellspacing="0">
<tr>
<th style="background: #FFFFFF; color: #000000; font-size: 8pt; padding: 0px 0px 0px 0px; text-align:center; vertical-align:top;"><b><a href="/wiki/List_of_Holy_Roman_Emperors" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Holy Roman Emperors">Holy Roman Emperor</a></b></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="background: #FFFFFF; padding: 0px 0px 8px 0px; text-align: center;"><a href="/wiki/File:Armoiries_Famille_Hohenstaufen.svg" class="image"><img alt="Armoiries Famille Hohenstaufen.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Armoiries_Famille_Hohenstaufen.svg/75px-Armoiries_Famille_Hohenstaufen.svg.png" width="75" height="83" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Armoiries_Famille_Hohenstaufen.svg/113px-Armoiries_Famille_Hohenstaufen.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Armoiries_Famille_Hohenstaufen.svg/150px-Armoiries_Famille_Hohenstaufen.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="660" /></a> <a href="/wiki/File:Holy_Roman_Empire_Arms-single_head.svg" class="image"><img alt="Holy Roman Empire Arms-single head.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Holy_Roman_Empire_Arms-single_head.svg/75px-Holy_Roman_Empire_Arms-single_head.svg.png" width="75" height="90" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Holy_Roman_Empire_Arms-single_head.svg/113px-Holy_Roman_Empire_Arms-single_head.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Holy_Roman_Empire_Arms-single_head.svg/150px-Holy_Roman_Empire_Arms-single_head.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="659" /></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<center>Coats of arms</center>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Otto_IV_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto IV of Germany">Otto of Brunswick</a> had been crowned Holy Roman Emperor by Pope Innocent III in 1209. In southern Italy, Otto became the champion of those noblemen and barons who feared Frederick's increasingly strong moves against them, exemplified by the firing of the pro-noble Walter of Palearia. The new emperor invaded Italy, where he reached Calabria without meeting much resistance. In response, Innocent sided against Otto, and in September 1211 at the <a href="/wiki/Diet_of_Nuremberg" title="Diet of Nuremberg">Diet of Nuremberg</a> Frederick was elected <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/in_absentia" class="extiw" title="wikt:in absentia">in absentia</a></i> as German King by a rebellious faction backed by the pope. Innocent also excommunicated Otto, who was forced to return to Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> Frederick sailed to <a href="/wiki/Gaeta" title="Gaeta">Gaeta</a> with a small following. He agreed with the pope on a future separation between the Sicilian and Imperial titles, and named his wife Constance as regent. Passing through <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engadin" title="Engadin">Engadin</a>, he reached <a href="/wiki/Konstanz" title="Konstanz">Konstanz</a> in September 1212, preceding Otto by a few hours.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frederick was crowned as king on 9 December 1212 in <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>. Frederick's authority in Germany remained tenuous, however, and he was recognized only in southern Germany; in the region of northern Germany, the center of <a href="/wiki/Guelphs_and_Ghibellines" title="Guelphs and Ghibellines">Guelph</a> power, Otto continued to hold the reins of royal and imperial power despite his excommunication. But Otto's decisive military defeat at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bouvines" title="Battle of Bouvines">Bouvines</a> forced him to withdraw to the Guelph hereditary lands where, virtually without supporters, he died in 1218. The German princes, supported by Innocent III, again elected Frederick king of Germany in 1215, and he was crowned king in <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a> on 23 July 1215 by one of the three German archbishops. It was not until another five years had passed, and only after further negotiations between Frederick, Innocent III, and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Honorius_III" title="Pope Honorius III">Honorius III</a> – who succeeded to the papacy after Innocent's death in 1216 – that Frederick was crowned Holy Roman Emperor in Rome by Honorius III, on 22 November 1220.<sup id="cite_ref-Toch381_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toch381-10">[10]</a></sup> At the same time, Frederick's oldest son <a href="/wiki/Henry_(VII)_of_Germany" title="Henry (VII) of Germany">Henry</a> took the title of King of the Romans.<sup id="cite_ref-Toch381_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Toch381-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gold_augustale_of_Emperor_Frederik_II_as_King_of_Sicily_1198_1250.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Gold_augustale_of_Emperor_Frederik_II_as_King_of_Sicily_1198_1250.jpg/300px-Gold_augustale_of_Emperor_Frederik_II_as_King_of_Sicily_1198_1250.jpg" width="300" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Gold_augustale_of_Emperor_Frederik_II_as_King_of_Sicily_1198_1250.jpg/450px-Gold_augustale_of_Emperor_Frederik_II_as_King_of_Sicily_1198_1250.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Gold_augustale_of_Emperor_Frederik_II_as_King_of_Sicily_1198_1250.jpg/600px-Gold_augustale_of_Emperor_Frederik_II_as_King_of_Sicily_1198_1250.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1142" data-file-height="604" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gold_augustale_of_Emperor_Frederik_II_as_King_of_Sicily_1198_1250.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gold <a href="/wiki/Augustale" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustale">augustale</a> of Emperor Frederik II, as King of Sicily 1198–1250.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Unlike most Holy Roman emperors, Frederick spent few years in Germany. In 1218, he helped <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_France" title="Philip II of France">Philip II of France</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eudes_III,_Duke_of_Burgundy" class="mw-redirect" title="Eudes III, Duke of Burgundy">Eudes III, Duke of Burgundy</a> to bring an end to the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Succession_(Champagne)" class="mw-redirect" title="War of Succession (Champagne)">War of Succession</a> in <a href="/wiki/Champagne,_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Champagne, France">Champagne</a> (France) by invading <a href="/wiki/Lorraine_(province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lorraine (province)">Lorraine</a>, capturing and burning <a href="/wiki/Nancy,_France" title="Nancy, France">Nancy</a>, capturing <a href="/wiki/Theobald_I,_Duke_of_Lorraine" title="Theobald I, Duke of Lorraine">Theobald I, Duke of Lorraine</a> and forcing him to withdraw his support from Erard of Brienne. After his coronation in 1220, Frederick remained either in the Kingdom of Sicily or on <a href="/wiki/Crusade" class="mw-redirect" title="Crusade">Crusade</a> until 1236, when he made his last journey to Germany. He returned to Italy in 1237 and stayed there for the remaining thirteen years of his life, represented in Germany by his son <a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">Conrad</a>.</p>
<p>In the Kingdom of Sicily, he built on the reform of the laws begun at the <a href="/wiki/Assizes_of_Ariano" title="Assizes of Ariano">Assizes of Ariano</a> in 1140 by his grandfather <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">Roger II</a>. His initiative in this direction was visible as early as the <a href="/wiki/Assizes_of_Capua" title="Assizes of Capua">Assizes of Capua</a> (1220, issued soon after his coronation in Rome) but came to fruition in his promulgation of the <a href="/wiki/Constitutions_of_Melfi" title="Constitutions of Melfi">Constitutions of Melfi</a> (1231, also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Liber_Augustalis" class="mw-redirect" title="Liber Augustalis">Liber Augustalis</a></i>), a collection of laws for his realm that was remarkable for its time and was a source of inspiration for a long time after. It made the Kingdom of Sicily an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolutist monarchy</a>; it also set a precedent for the primacy of written law. With relatively small modifications, the <i>Liber Augustalis</i> remained the basis of Sicilian law until 1819.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Fifth_Crusade_and_early_policies_in_northern_Italy">The Fifth Crusade and early policies in northern Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The Fifth Crusade and early policies in northern Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>At the time he was elected King of the Romans, Frederick promised to go on <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Crusade" title="Fifth Crusade">crusade</a>. He continually delayed, however, and, in spite of his renewal of this vow at his coronation as the King of Germany, he did not travel to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> with the armies of the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Crusade" title="Fifth Crusade">Fifth Crusade</a> in 1217. He sent forces to Egypt under the command of <a href="/wiki/Louis_I,_Duke_of_Bavaria" title="Louis I, Duke of Bavaria">Louis I, Duke of Bavaria</a>, but constant expectation of his arrival caused papal legate <a href="/wiki/Pelagio_Galvani" title="Pelagio Galvani">Pelagius</a> to reject <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayyubid">Ayyubid</a> sultan <a href="/wiki/Al-Kamil" title="Al-Kamil">Al-Kamil</a>'s offer to restore the Latin Kingdom of Jerusalem to the crusaders in exchange for their withdrawal from Egypt and caused the Crusade to continually stall in anticipation of his ever-delayed arrival. The crusade ended in failure with the loss of <a href="/wiki/Damietta" title="Damietta">Damietta</a> in 1221.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Frederick was blamed by both Pope <a href="/wiki/Honorius_III" class="mw-redirect" title="Honorius III">Honorius III</a> and the general Christian populace for this calamitous defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1225, after agreeing with Pope Honorius to launch a Crusade [but?] not [until?] after 1227, Frederick summoned an imperial Diet at <a href="/wiki/Cremona" title="Cremona">Cremona</a>, the main pro-imperial city in <a href="/wiki/Lombardy" title="Lombardy">Lombardy</a>: the main arguments for holding the Diet would be to continue the struggle against heresy, to organize the crusade and, above all, to restore the imperial power in northern Italy, which had been long been usurped by the numerous <a href="/wiki/Medieval_communes" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval communes">communes</a> located there. Those assembled responded with the reformation of the <a href="/wiki/Lombard_League" title="Lombard League">Lombard League</a>, which had already defeated his grandfather <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Barbarossa" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick Barbarossa">Frederick Barbarossa</a> in the 12th century, and again <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a> was chosen as the league's leader. The Diet was cancelled, however, and the situation was stabilized only through a compromise reached by Honorius between Frederick and the League.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> During his sojourn in northern Italy, Frederick also invested the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Order</a> with the territories in what would become <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a>, starting what was later called the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusade" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Crusade">Northern Crusade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sixth_Crusade">The Sixth Crusade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: The Sixth Crusade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Crusade" title="Sixth Crusade">Sixth Crusade</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:193px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Kamil_Muhammad_al-Malik_and_Frederick_II_Holy_Roman_Emperor.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7c/Al-Kamil_Muhammad_al-Malik_and_Frederick_II_Holy_Roman_Emperor.jpg" width="191" height="393" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="191" data-file-height="393" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Al-Kamil_Muhammad_al-Malik_and_Frederick_II_Holy_Roman_Emperor.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Frederick II (left) meets <a href="/wiki/Al-Kamil" title="Al-Kamil">Al-Kamil</a> (right).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Problems of stability within the empire delayed Frederick's departure on crusade. It was not until 1225, when, by proxy, Frederick had married <a href="/wiki/Yolande_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Yolande of Jerusalem">Yolande of Jerusalem</a>, heiress to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a>, that his departure seemed assured. Frederick immediately saw to it that his new father-in-law <a href="/wiki/John_of_Brienne" title="John of Brienne">John of Brienne</a>, the current king of Jerusalem, was dispossessed and his rights transferred to the emperor. In August 1227, Frederick set out for the Holy Land from <a href="/wiki/Brindisi" title="Brindisi">Brindisi</a> but was forced to return when he was struck down by an epidemic that had broken out. Even the master of the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermann_of_Salza" class="mw-redirect" title="Hermann of Salza">Hermann of Salza</a>, recommended that he return to the mainland to recuperate. On 29 September 1227, Frederick was excommunicated by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_IX" title="Pope Gregory IX">Pope Gregory IX</a> for failing to honor his crusading pledge.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many contemporary chroniclers doubted the sincerity of Frederick's illness, and their attitude may be explained by their pro-papal leanings. <a href="/wiki/Roger_of_Wendover" title="Roger of Wendover">Roger of Wendover</a>, a chronicler of the time, wrote:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>... he went to the Mediterranean sea, and embarked with a small retinue; but after pretending to make for the holy land for three days, he said that he was seized with a sudden illness… this conduct of the emperor redounded much to his disgrace, and to the injury of the whole business of the crusade.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Frederick eventually sailed again from Brindisi in June 1228. The pope, still Gregory IX, regarded that action as a provocation, since, as an excommunicate, Frederick was technically not capable of conducting a Crusade, and he excommunicated the emperor a second time. Frederick reached <a href="/wiki/Acre_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Acre (city)">Acre</a> in September. Since all the local authorities and most of the military orders denied him any help, and because the crusading army was a meagre force, Frederick negotiated along the lines of a previous agreement he had intended to broker with the <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayyubid">Ayyubid</a> sultan, <a href="/wiki/Al-Kamil" title="Al-Kamil">Al-Kamil</a>. The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Treaty_of_Jaffa_and_Tell_Ajul&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Treaty of Jaffa and Tell Ajul (page does not exist)">treaty</a>, signed in February 1229, resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Restitution" title="Restitution">restitution</a> of Jerusalem, <a href="/wiki/Nazareth" title="Nazareth">Nazareth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">Bethlehem</a>, and a small coastal strip to the Kingdom of Jerusalem, though there are disagreements as to the extent of the territory returned.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The treaty also stipulated that the <a href="/wiki/Dome_of_the_Rock" title="Dome of the Rock">Dome of the Rock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Aqsa_Mosque" title="Al-Aqsa Mosque">al-Aqsa Mosque</a> were to remain under Muslim control and that the city of Jerusalem would remain without fortifications.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> Virtually all other crusaders, including the Templars and Hospitallers, condemned this deal as a political ploy on the part of Frederick to regain his kingdom while betraying the cause of the Crusaders. Al-Kamil, who was nervous about possible war with his relatives who ruled <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, wished to avoid further trouble from the Christians, at least until his domestic rivals were subdued.</p>
<p>The crusade ended in a <a href="/wiki/Truce" class="mw-redirect" title="Truce">truce</a> and in Frederick's <a href="/wiki/Coronation" title="Coronation">coronation</a> as <a href="/wiki/Kings_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings of Jerusalem">King of Jerusalem</a> on 18 March 1229, although this was technically improper. Frederick's wife Yolande, the heiress, had died, leaving their infant son <a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">Conrad</a> as rightful king. There is also disagreement as to whether the "coronation" was a coronation at all, as a letter written by Frederick to <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III of England</a> suggests that the crown he placed on his own head was in fact the imperial crown of the Romans.</p>
<p>In any case, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Gerald_of_Lausanne&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Gerald of Lausanne (page does not exist)">Gerald of Lausanne</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Patriarch_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin Patriarch of Jerusalem">Latin Patriarch of Jerusalem</a>, did not attend the ceremony; indeed, the next day the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Caesarea" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Caesarea">Bishop of Caesarea</a> arrived to place the city under interdict on the patriarch's orders. Frederick's further attempts to rule over the Kingdom of Jerusalem were met by resistance on the part of the barons, led by <a href="/wiki/John_of_Ibelin,_the_Old_Lord_of_Beirut" title="John of Ibelin, the Old Lord of Beirut">John of Ibelin, Lord of Beirut</a>. In the mid-1230s, Frederick's viceroy was forced to leave Acre, and in 1244, following a <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(1244)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (1244)">siege</a>, Jerusalem itself was lost again to a new Muslim offensive.</p>
<p>Whilst Frederick's seeming bloodless recovery of Jerusalem for the cross brought him great prestige in some European circles, his decision to complete the crusade while excommunicated provoked Church hostility. Although in 1230 the Pope lifted Frederick's excommunication at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ceprano_(1230)" title="Treaty of Ceprano (1230)">Treaty of Ceprano</a>, this decision was taken for a variety of reasons related to the political situation in Europe. Of Frederick's crusade, <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Novara" title="Philip of Novara">Philip of Novara</a>, a chronicler of the period, said, "The emperor left Acre [after the conclusion of the truce]; hated, cursed, and vilified."<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Overall this crusade, arguably the first successful one since the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a>, was adversely affected by the manner in which Frederick carried out negotiations without the support of the church. He left behind a kingdom in the Levant torn between his agents and the local nobility, a civil war known as the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Lombards" title="War of the Lombards">War of the Lombards</a>.</p>
<p>The itinerant <a href="/wiki/Joachimite" class="mw-redirect" title="Joachimite">Joachimite</a> preachers and many radical <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">Franciscans</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Franciscan_Spirituals" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan Spirituals">Spirituals</a>, supported Frederick. Against the interdict pronounced on his lands, the preachers condemned the Pope and continued to minister the sacraments and grant absolutions. Brother Arnold in <a href="/wiki/Swabia" title="Swabia">Swabia</a> proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a> for 1260, at which time Frederick would then confiscate the riches of Rome and distribute them among the poor, the "only true Christians."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="The_war_against_the_Pope_and_Henry's_revolt"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_war_against_the_Pope_and_Henry.27s_revolt">The war against the Pope and Henry's revolt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: The war against the Pope and Henry's revolt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChronicaMajora-Giglio1241-MatthewParis-HenryIII(1259).PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/ChronicaMajora-Giglio1241-MatthewParis-HenryIII%281259%29.PNG/260px-ChronicaMajora-Giglio1241-MatthewParis-HenryIII%281259%29.PNG" width="260" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/ChronicaMajora-Giglio1241-MatthewParis-HenryIII%281259%29.PNG/390px-ChronicaMajora-Giglio1241-MatthewParis-HenryIII%281259%29.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/ChronicaMajora-Giglio1241-MatthewParis-HenryIII%281259%29.PNG/520px-ChronicaMajora-Giglio1241-MatthewParis-HenryIII%281259%29.PNG 2x" data-file-width="651" data-file-height="244" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChronicaMajora-Giglio1241-MatthewParis-HenryIII(1259).PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Giglio_(1241)" title="Battle of Giglio (1241)">Battle of Giglio</a>, against <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_IX" title="Pope Gregory IX">Gregory IX</a> (1241)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During Frederick's stay in the Holy Land, his regent, Rainald of Spoleto, had attacked the <a href="/wiki/Marche" title="Marche">Marche</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Spoleto" title="Duchy of Spoleto">Duchy of Spoleto</a>. Gregory IX recruited an army under <a href="/wiki/John_of_Brienne" title="John of Brienne">John of Brienne</a> and, in 1229, invaded southern Italy. His troops overcame an initial resistance at <a href="/wiki/Montecassino" class="mw-redirect" title="Montecassino">Montecassino</a> and reached <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a>. Frederick arrived at Brindisi in June 1229. He quickly recovered the lost territories and trialled the rebel barons, but avoided crossing the boundaries with the Papal States.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> The war came to an end with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ceprano_(1230)" title="Treaty of Ceprano (1230)">Treaty of Ceprano</a> in the summer of 1230; the emperor personally met Gregory IX at <a href="/wiki/Anagni" title="Anagni">Anagni</a>, making some concessions to the church in Sicily.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> He also issued the <a href="/wiki/Constitutions_of_Melfi" title="Constitutions of Melfi">Constitutions of Melfi</a> (August 1231), as an attempt to solve the political and administrative problems of the country, which had dramatically been shown by the recent war.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>While he may have temporarily made his peace with the pope, Frederick found the German princes another matter. Frederick's son <a href="/wiki/Henry_(VII)_of_Germany" title="Henry (VII) of Germany">Henry VII</a> (who was born 1211 in Sicily, son of Frederick's first wife <a href="/wiki/Constance_of_Aragon" title="Constance of Aragon">Constance of Aragon</a>) had caused their discontent with an aggressive policy against their privileges. This forced Henry to a complete capitulation, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Statutum_in_favorem_principum" title="Statutum in favorem principum">Statutum in favorem principum</a></i> ("Statutes in favor of the princes"), issued at Worms, deprived the emperor of much of his sovereignty in Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> Frederick summoned Henry to a meeting, which was held at <a href="/wiki/Aquileia" title="Aquileia">Aquileia</a> in 1232. Henry confirmed his submission, but Frederick was nevertheless compelled to confirm the <i>Statutum</i> at <a href="/wiki/Cividale" class="mw-redirect" title="Cividale">Cividale</a> soon afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The situation for Frederick was also problematic in Lombardy, after all the emperor's attempts to restore the imperial authority in Lombardy with the help of Gregory IX (at the time, ousted from Rome by a revolt) turned to nothing in 1233. In the meantime Henry in Germany had returned to an anti-princes policy, against his father's will: Frederick thus obtained his excommunication from Gregory IX (July 1234). Henry tried to muster an opposition in Germany and asked the Lombard cities to block the Alpine passes. In May 1235, Frederick went to Germany, taking no army with him: as soon as July, however, he was able to force his son to renounce to the crown all his lands, at Worms, and then imprisoned him.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Germany the Hohenstaufen and the Guelphs reconciled in 1235. <a href="/wiki/Otto_the_Child" class="mw-redirect" title="Otto the Child">Otto the Child</a>, the grandson of <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Lion" title="Henry the Lion">Henry the Lion</a>, had been deposed as Duke of <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Bavaria" title="Duchy of Bavaria">Bavaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Saxony" title="Duchy of Saxony">Saxony</a> in 1180, conveying the allodial Guelphic possessions to Frederick, who in return <a href="/wiki/Enfeoffed" class="mw-redirect" title="Enfeoffed">enfeoffed</a> Otto with the same lands and additional former imperial possessions as the newly established Duke of <a href="/wiki/Brunswick-L%C3%BCneburg" class="mw-redirect" title="Brunswick-Lüneburg">Brunswick-Lüneburg</a>, ending the unclear status of the German Guelphs, who had been left without title and rank after 1180.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="War_in_Lombardy">War in Lombardy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: War in Lombardy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cortenuova1237.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Cortenuova1237.JPG/260px-Cortenuova1237.JPG" width="260" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Cortenuova1237.JPG/390px-Cortenuova1237.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e4/Cortenuova1237.JPG 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="294" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cortenuova1237.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The victorious <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cortenuova" title="Battle of Cortenuova">Battle of Cortenuova</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Lombard_League" title="Lombard League">2nd Lombard League</a> (1237)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Federico_II_platba.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Federico_II_platba.jpg/260px-Federico_II_platba.jpg" width="260" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Federico_II_platba.jpg/390px-Federico_II_platba.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Federico_II_platba.jpg/520px-Federico_II_platba.jpg 2x" data-file-width="543" data-file-height="376" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Federico_II_platba.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Frederick II's troops paid with leather coins during the sieges of <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Brescia" title="Siege of Brescia">Brescia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Faenza" title="Siege of Faenza">Faenza</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-WIP_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WIP-16">[16]</a></sup> <i>Chigi Codex</i> - <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Library" title="Vatican Library">Vatican Library</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With peace north of the Alps, Frederick raised an army from the German princes to suppress the rebel cities in Lombardy. Gregory tried to stop the invasion with diplomatic moves, but in vain. During his descent to Italy, Frederick had to divert his troops to quell a rebellion of <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Duke_of_Austria" title="Frederick II, Duke of Austria">Frederick II, Duke of Austria</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, in February 1237, he obtained the title of <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Romans" title="King of the Romans">King of the Romans</a> for his 9-year-old son <a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">Conrad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the failure of the negotiations between the Lombard cities, the pope and the imperial diplomats, Frederick invaded Lombardy from <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>. In November 1237 he won the decisive <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cortenuova" title="Battle of Cortenuova">battle in Cortenuova</a> over the Lombard League. Frederick celebrated it with a triumph in <a href="/wiki/Cremona" title="Cremona">Cremona</a> in the manner of an ancient <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a>, with the captured <i><a href="/wiki/Carroccio" title="Carroccio">carroccio</a></i> (later sent to the commune of Rome) and an elephant. He rejected any suit for peace, even from <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, which had sent a great sum of money. This demand of total surrender spurred further resistance from Milan, <a href="/wiki/Brescia" title="Brescia">Brescia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Piacenza" title="Piacenza">Piacenza</a>, and in October 1238 he was forced to raise the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Brescia" title="Siege of Brescia">siege of Brescia</a>, in the course of which his enemies had tried unsuccessfully to capture him.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frederick received the news of his excommunication by Gregory IX in the first months of 1239<sup id="cite_ref-bressler_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bressler-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:149</sup> while his court was in <a href="/wiki/Padua" title="Padua">Padua</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. The emperor responded by expelling the <a href="/wiki/Minorite" class="mw-redirect" title="Minorite">Franciscans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominicans</a> from Lombardy and electing his son <a href="/wiki/Enzo_of_Sardinia" title="Enzo of Sardinia">Enzo</a> as Imperial vicar for Northern Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-csun_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-csun-18">[18]</a></sup> Enzo soon annexed the <a href="/wiki/Romagna" title="Romagna">Romagna</a>, <a href="/wiki/March_of_Ancona" title="March of Ancona">Marche</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Spoleto" title="Duchy of Spoleto">Duchy of Spoleto</a>, nominally part of the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a>. The father announced he was to destroy the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Republic of Venice</a>, which had sent some ships against Sicily. In December of that year Frederick marched over <a href="/wiki/Tuscany" title="Tuscany">Tuscany</a>, entered triumphantly into <a href="/wiki/Foligno" title="Foligno">Foligno</a>, and then in <a href="/wiki/Viterbo" title="Viterbo">Viterbo</a>, whence he aimed to finally conquer Rome to restore the ancient splendours of the Empire. The siege, however, was ineffective, and Frederick returned to Southern Italy, sacking <a href="/wiki/Benevento" title="Benevento">Benevento</a> (a papal possession). Peace negotiations came to nothing.</p>
<p>In the meantime the <a href="/wiki/Ghibelline" class="mw-redirect" title="Ghibelline">Ghibelline</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Ferrara" title="Ferrara">Ferrara</a> had fallen, and Frederick swept his way northwards capturing <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> and, after <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Faenza" title="Siege of Faenza">another long siege</a>, <a href="/wiki/Faenza" title="Faenza">Faenza</a>. The people of <a href="/wiki/Forl%C3%AC" title="Forlì">Forlì</a>, which had kept its Ghibelline stance even after the collapse of <a href="/wiki/House_of_Hohenstaufen" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufen</a> power, offered their loyal support during the capture of the rival city: as a sign of gratitude, they were granted an augmentation of the communal coat-of-arms with the Hohenstaufen eagle, together with other privileges. This episode shows how the independent cities used the rivalry between Empire and Pope as a means to obtain maximum advantage for themselves.</p>
<p>The Pope called a council, but Ghibelline <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Pisa" title="Republic of Pisa">Pisa</a> thwarted it, <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Meloria_(1241)" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Meloria (1241)">capturing cardinals and prelates</a> on a ship sailing from <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a> to Rome. Frederick thought that this time the way into Rome was opened, and he again directed his forces against the Pope, leaving behind him a ruined and burning <a href="/wiki/Umbria" title="Umbria">Umbria</a>. Frederick destroyed <a href="/wiki/Grottaferrata" title="Grottaferrata">Grottaferrata</a> preparing to invade Rome. Then, on 22 August 1241, Gregory died. Frederick, showing that his war was not directed against the Church of Rome but against the Pope, drew back his troops and freed two cardinals from the jail of <a href="/wiki/Capua" title="Capua">Capua</a>. Nothing changed in the relationship between Papacy and Empire, however, as Roman troops assaulted the Imperial garrison in <a href="/wiki/Tivoli,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Tivoli, Italy">Tivoli</a> and the Emperor soon reached Rome. This back-and-forth situation was repeated again in 1242 and 1243.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Innocent_IV">Innocent IV</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Innocent IV">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ad_Apostolicae_Dignitatis_Apicem" title="Ad Apostolicae Dignitatis Apicem">Ad Apostolicae Dignitatis Apicem</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castel_del_Monte_BW_2016-10-14_12-26-11_r.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Castel_del_Monte_BW_2016-10-14_12-26-11_r.jpg/250px-Castel_del_Monte_BW_2016-10-14_12-26-11_r.jpg" width="250" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Castel_del_Monte_BW_2016-10-14_12-26-11_r.jpg/375px-Castel_del_Monte_BW_2016-10-14_12-26-11_r.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Castel_del_Monte_BW_2016-10-14_12-26-11_r.jpg/500px-Castel_del_Monte_BW_2016-10-14_12-26-11_r.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5283" data-file-height="3861" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castel_del_Monte_BW_2016-10-14_12-26-11_r.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Castel_del_Monte_(Apulia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Castel del Monte (Apulia)">Castel del Monte</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Andria,_Italy" class="mw-redirect" title="Andria, Italy">Andria</a>, Apulia, Italy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A new pope, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Innocent IV</a>, was elected on 25 June 1243. He was a member of a noble Imperial family and had some relatives in Frederick's camp, so the Emperor was initially happy with his election. Innocent, however, was to become his fiercest enemy. Negotiations began in the summer of 1243, but the situation changed as <a href="/wiki/Viterbo" title="Viterbo">Viterbo</a> rebelled, instigated by the intriguing local cardinal <a href="/wiki/Ranieri_Capocci" class="mw-redirect" title="Ranieri Capocci">Ranieri Capocci</a>. Frederick could not afford to lose his main stronghold near Rome, so he <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Viterbo" title="Siege of Viterbo">besieged the city</a>. Innocent convinced the rebels to sign a peace but, after Frederick withdrew his garrison, Ranieri nonetheless had them slaughtered on 13 November. Frederick was enraged. The new Pope was a master diplomat, and Frederick signed a peace treaty, which was soon broken. Innocent showed his true Guelph face, and, together with most of the Cardinals, fled via Genoese galleys to <a href="/wiki/Liguria" title="Liguria">Liguria</a>, arriving on 7 July. His aim was to reach <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a>, where a new council was being held since 24 June 1245. Despite initially appearing that the council could end with a compromise, the intervention of Ranieri, who had a series of insulting pamphlets published against Frederick (in which, among other things, he defined the emperor as a heretic and an Antichrist), led the prelates towards a less accommodating solution.<sup id="cite_ref-Kamp_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kamp-19">[19]</a></sup> One month later, Innocent IV declared Frederick to be deposed as emperor, characterising him as a "friend of Babylon's sultan," "of Saracen customs," "provided with a harem guarded by <a href="/wiki/Eunuchs" class="mw-redirect" title="Eunuchs">eunuchs</a>," like the schismatic emperor of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantium</a>, and in sum a "heretic."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Pope backed <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Raspe" class="mw-redirect" title="Heinrich Raspe">Heinrich Raspe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Landgrave_of_Thuringia" class="mw-redirect" title="Landgrave of Thuringia">landgrave of Thuringia</a>, as rival for the imperial crown and set in motion a plot to kill Frederick and Enzo, with the support of the pope's brother-in-law Orlando de Rossi, another friend of Frederick. The plotters were unmasked by the count of <a href="/wiki/Caserta" title="Caserta">Caserta</a>, however, and the city of <a href="/wiki/Altavilla_Silentina" title="Altavilla Silentina">Altavilla</a>, where they had found shelter, was razed. The guilty were blinded, mutilated, and burnt alive or hanged. An attempt to invade the Kingdom of Sicily, under the command of Ranieri, was halted at <a href="/wiki/Spello" title="Spello">Spello</a> by Marino of Eboli, Imperial vicar of Spoleto.</p>
<p>Innocent also sent a flow of money to Germany to cut off Frederick's power at its source. The <a href="/wiki/Archbishops_of_Cologne" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishops of Cologne">archbishops of Cologne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Mainz" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishop of Mainz">Mainz</a> also declared Frederick deposed, and in May 1246 Heinrich Raspe was chosen as the new king. On 5 August 1246 Heinrich, thanks to the Pope's money, managed to defeat an army of Conrad, son of Frederick, near <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a>. Frederick strengthened his position in Southern Germany, however, acquiring the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Austria" title="Duchy of Austria">Duchy of Austria</a>, whose duke had died without heirs. A year later Heinrich died, and the new <a href="/wiki/Anti-king" title="Anti-king">anti-king</a> was <a href="/wiki/William_II,_Count_of_Holland" class="mw-redirect" title="William II, Count of Holland">William II, Count of Holland</a>.</p>
<p>Between February and March 1247 Frederick settled the situation in Italy by means of the diet of <a href="/wiki/Terni" title="Terni">Terni</a>, naming his relatives or friends as vicars of the various lands. He married his son Manfred to the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Amadeus_IV_of_Savoy" class="mw-redirect" title="Amadeus IV of Savoy">Amedeo di Savoia</a> and secured the submission of the marquis of <a href="/wiki/Monferrato" class="mw-redirect" title="Monferrato">Monferrato</a>. On his part, Innocent asked protection from the King of France, <a href="/wiki/Louis_IX_of_France" title="Louis IX of France">Louis IX</a>, but the king was a friend of the Emperor and believed in his desire for peace. A papal army under the command of <a href="/wiki/Ottaviano_degli_Ubaldini" title="Ottaviano degli Ubaldini">Ottaviano degli Ubaldini</a> never reached Lombardy, and the Emperor, accompanied by a massive army, held the next diet in <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Battle_of_Parma">Battle of Parma</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Battle of Parma">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(March 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Federico_II_Parma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Federico_II_Parma.jpg/250px-Federico_II_Parma.jpg" width="250" height="188" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/13/Federico_II_Parma.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="368" data-file-height="276" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The unexpected sally of the <a href="/wiki/Guelph" title="Guelph">Guelph</a> cavalry from Parma against Vittoria, from a medieval manuscript</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Enzo_Codice_Chigi.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Enzo_Codice_Chigi.JPG/250px-Enzo_Codice_Chigi.JPG" width="250" height="234" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Enzo_Codice_Chigi.JPG/375px-Enzo_Codice_Chigi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Enzo_Codice_Chigi.JPG 2x" data-file-width="402" data-file-height="376" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fossalta" title="Battle of Fossalta">Battle of Fossalta</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Lombard_League" title="Lombard League">2nd Lombard League</a> (1249)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An unexpected event was to change the situation dramatically. In June 1247 the important Lombard city of Parma expelled the Imperial functionaries and sided with the Guelphs. Enzo was not in the city and could do nothing more than ask for help from his father, who came back to lay siege to the rebels, together with his friend <a href="/wiki/Ezzelino_III_da_Romano" title="Ezzelino III da Romano">Ezzelino III da Romano</a>, tyrant of <a href="/wiki/Verona" title="Verona">Verona</a>. The besieged languished as the Emperor waited for them to surrender from starvation. He had a wooden city, which he called "Vittoria", built around the walls.</p>
<p>On 18 February 1248, during one of these absences, the camp was suddenly assaulted and taken, and in the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Parma" title="Battle of Parma">Battle of Parma</a> the Imperial side was routed. Frederick lost the Imperial treasure and with it any hope of maintaining the impetus of his struggle against the rebellious communes and against the pope, who began plans for a crusade against Sicily. Frederick soon recovered and rebuilt an army, but this defeat encouraged resistance in many cities that could no longer bear the fiscal burden of his regime: Romagna, Marche and Spoleto were lost.</p>
<p>In February 1249 Frederick fired his advisor and prime minister, the famous jurist and poet <a href="/wiki/Pier_delle_Vigne" class="mw-redirect" title="Pier delle Vigne">Pier delle Vigne</a>, on charges of peculation and <a href="/wiki/Embezzlement" title="Embezzlement">embezzlement</a>. Some historians suggest that Pier was planning to betray the Emperor, who, according to <a href="/wiki/Matthew_of_Paris" class="mw-redirect" title="Matthew of Paris">Matthew of Paris</a>, cried when he discovered the plot. Pier, blinded and in chains, died in Pisa, possibly by his own hand. Even more shocking for Frederick was the capture of his natural son <a href="/wiki/Enzo_of_Sardinia" title="Enzo of Sardinia">Enzo of Sardinia</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bolognese</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Fossalta" title="Battle of Fossalta">Battle of Fossalta</a>, in May, 1249. Enzo was held in a palace in Bologna, where he remained captive until his death in 1272.</p>
<p>Frederick lost another son, Richard of <a href="/wiki/Chieti" title="Chieti">Chieti</a>. The struggle continued: the Empire lost <a href="/wiki/Como" title="Como">Como</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modena" title="Modena">Modena</a>, but regained <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a>. An army sent to invade the Kingdom of Sicily under the command of Cardinal Pietro Capocci was crushed in the Marche at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cingoli" title="Battle of Cingoli">Battle of Cingoli</a> in 1250. In the first month of that year the indomitable Ranieri of Viterbo died and the Imperial <i>condottieri</i> again reconquered Romagna, the Marche and Spoleto; and <a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">Conrad, King of the Romans</a>, scored several victories in Germany against William of Holland.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:157px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor_-_Cathedral_of_Palermo_-_Italy_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tomb_of_Frederick_II%2C_Holy_Roman_Emperor_-_Cathedral_of_Palermo_-_Italy_2015.jpg/155px-Tomb_of_Frederick_II%2C_Holy_Roman_Emperor_-_Cathedral_of_Palermo_-_Italy_2015.jpg" width="155" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tomb_of_Frederick_II%2C_Holy_Roman_Emperor_-_Cathedral_of_Palermo_-_Italy_2015.jpg/233px-Tomb_of_Frederick_II%2C_Holy_Roman_Emperor_-_Cathedral_of_Palermo_-_Italy_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Tomb_of_Frederick_II%2C_Holy_Roman_Emperor_-_Cathedral_of_Palermo_-_Italy_2015.jpg/310px-Tomb_of_Frederick_II%2C_Holy_Roman_Emperor_-_Cathedral_of_Palermo_-_Italy_2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="3072" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor_-_Cathedral_of_Palermo_-_Italy_2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The sarcophagus of Frederick II in the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Palermo" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of Palermo">Cathedral of Palermo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Frederick did not take part in of any of these campaigns. He had been ill and likely felt tired. Despite the betrayals and the setbacks he had faced in his last years, Frederick died peacefully, wearing the habit of a <a href="/wiki/Cistercian" class="mw-redirect" title="Cistercian">Cistercian</a> monk, on 13 December 1250 in <a href="/wiki/Castel_Fiorentino" class="mw-redirect" title="Castel Fiorentino">Castel Fiorentino</a> (territory of <a href="/wiki/Torremaggiore" title="Torremaggiore">Torremaggiore</a>), in <a href="/wiki/Apulia" title="Apulia">Apulia</a>, after an attack of <a href="/wiki/Dysentery" title="Dysentery">dysentery</a>.</p>
<p>At the time of his death, his preeminent position in Europe was challenged but not lost: his testament left his legitimate son Conrad the Imperial and Sicilian crowns. Manfred received the principality of <a href="/wiki/Taranto" title="Taranto">Taranto</a> and the government of the Kingdom, Henry the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Arles" title="Kingdom of Arles">Kingdom of Arles</a> or that of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, while the son of <a href="/wiki/Henry_(VII)_of_Germany" title="Henry (VII) of Germany">Henry VII</a> was entrusted with the Duchy of Austria and the <a href="/wiki/March_of_Styria" title="March of Styria">March of Styria</a>. Frederick's will stipulated that all the lands he had taken from the Church were to be returned to it, all the prisoners freed, and the taxes reduced, provided this did not damage the Empire's prestige.</p>
<p>However, upon Conrad's death a mere four years later, the Hohenstaufen dynasty fell from power and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire#Interregnum" title="Holy Roman Empire">Great Interregnum</a> began, lasting until 1273, one year after the last Hohenstaufen, Enzo, had died in his prison. During this time, a legend developed that Frederick was <a href="/wiki/King_in_the_mountain" title="King in the mountain">not truly dead but merely sleeping</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Kyffh%C3%A4user" title="Kyffhäuser">Kyffhäuser</a> Mountains and would one day awaken to reestablish his empire. Over time, this legend largely transferred itself to his grandfather, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I</a>, also known as <i>Barbarossa</i> ("Redbeard").<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (August 2014)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>His <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> (made of red <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(geology)" title="Porphyry (geology)">porphyry</a>) lies in the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Palermo" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of Palermo">cathedral of Palermo</a> beside those of his parents (Henry VI and Constance) as well as his grandfather, the <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman</a> king <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">Roger II of Sicily</a>. A bust of Frederick sits in the <a href="/wiki/Walhalla_temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Walhalla temple">Walhalla temple</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_I_of_Bavaria" title="Ludwig I of Bavaria">Ludwig I of Bavaria</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Personality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:209px;max-width:209px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:207px;max-width:207px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Federico_II_di_Svevia,_Statua_Piazza_Plebiscito.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Federico_II_di_Svevia%2C_Statua_Piazza_Plebiscito.png/205px-Federico_II_di_Svevia%2C_Statua_Piazza_Plebiscito.png" width="205" height="308" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Federico_II_di_Svevia%2C_Statua_Piazza_Plebiscito.png/308px-Federico_II_di_Svevia%2C_Statua_Piazza_Plebiscito.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f4/Federico_II_di_Svevia%2C_Statua_Piazza_Plebiscito.png 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="481" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Statue of Frederick II at the <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Reale,_Naples" class="mw-redirect" title="Palazzo Reale, Naples">Palazzo Reale, Naples</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:207px;max-width:207px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Castel_del_Monte_entrance_detail2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Castel_del_Monte_entrance_detail2.jpg/205px-Castel_del_Monte_entrance_detail2.jpg" width="205" height="156" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Castel_del_Monte_entrance_detail2.jpg/308px-Castel_del_Monte_entrance_detail2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Castel_del_Monte_entrance_detail2.jpg/410px-Castel_del_Monte_entrance_detail2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1458" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Detail of the <a href="/wiki/Castel_del_Monte,_Apulia" title="Castel del Monte, Apulia">Castel del Monte</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Frederick's contemporaries called him <i>stupor mundi</i>, the "astonishment of the world";<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> the majority of his contemporaries were indeed astonished—and sometimes repelled—by the pronounced unorthodoxy of the Hohenstaufen emperor and his temperamental stubbornness.<sup id="cite_ref-Cattaneo_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cattaneo-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frederick inherited German, Norman, and Sicilian blood, but by training, lifestyle, and temperament he was "most of all Sicilian."<sup id="cite_ref-Maehl_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maehl-23">[23]</a></sup> Maehl concludes that "To the end of his life he remained above all a Sicilian <i>grand signore</i>, and his whole imperial policy aimed at expanding the Sicilian kingdom into Italy rather than the German kingdom southward."<sup id="cite_ref-Maehl_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maehl-23">[23]</a></sup> Cantor concludes that "Frederick had no intention of giving up Naples and Sicily, which were the real strongholds of its power. He was, in fact, uninterested in Germany."<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frederick was a religious sceptic.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> Despite accusations of <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">blasphemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">paganism</a>, and the presence of pagan and oriental elements in his imperial conceptions, Frederick remained substantially linked to traditional Christianity, as shown by his early contacts with both the Franciscans and the Cistercians (in 1215 he was admitted to that order's praying community), as well as with <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_Hungary" title="Elizabeth of Hungary">St Elizabeth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> In spite of this, Frederick's religious scepticism was unusual for the era in which he lived, and to his contemporaries was highly shocking and scandalous. His papal enemies used it against him at every turn; he was subsequently referred to as <i>preambulus Antichristi</i> (predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">Antichrist</a>) by Pope Gregory IX, and, as Frederick allegedly did not respect the <i>privilegium potestatis</i> of the Church, he was excommunicated.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a>, where the three-year-old boy was brought after his mother's death, he was said to have grown up like a street youth. He was highly precocious, but the only benefit from Innocent III's guardianship was that at fourteen years of age he married a twenty-five-year-old widow named Constance, the daughter of the king of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">Aragon</a>. Both seem to have been happy with the arrangement, and Constance soon bore a son, Henry.</p>
<p>At his coronation, he may have worn the red silk mantle that had been crafted during the reign of Roger II. It bore an Arabic inscription indicating that the robe dated from the year 528 in the Muslim calendar, and incorporated a generic benediction, wishing its wearer "vast prosperity, great generosity and high splendor, fame and magnificent endowments, and the fulfillment of his wishes and hopes. May his days and nights go in pleasure without end or change." This coronation robe can be found today in the <i>Schatzkammer</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Kunsthistorisches_Museum" title="Kunsthistorisches Museum">Kunsthistorisches Museum</a> in Vienna.</p>
<p>Rather than exterminate the Muslim population of Western Sicily, he deported them at <a href="/wiki/Muslim_settlement_of_Lucera" title="Muslim settlement of Lucera">Lucera</a>. Not least, he enlisted them in his Christian army and even into his personal bodyguards. As Muslim soldiers, they had the advantage of immunity from papal excommunication. For these reasons, as well as his supposed <a href="/wiki/Epicureanism" title="Epicureanism">Epicureanism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Frederick II is listed as a representative member of the sixth region of <a href="/wiki/Inferno_(Dante)" title="Inferno (Dante)">Dante's Inferno</a>, that of the heretics, who are burned in tombs.</p>
<p>A further example of how much Frederick differed from his contemporaries was the conduct of his Crusade in the Holy Land. Outside <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a>, with the power to take it, he parleyed five months with the <a href="/wiki/Ayyubid" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayyubid">Ayyubid</a> <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> of Egypt <a href="/wiki/Al-Kamil" title="Al-Kamil">al-Kamil</a> about the surrender of the city. The Sultan summoned him into Jerusalem and entertained him in the most lavish fashion. When the muezzin, out of consideration for Frederick, failed to make the morning call to prayer, the emperor declared: "I stayed overnight in Jerusalem, in order to overhear the prayer call of the Muslims and their worthy God." The Saracens had a good opinion of him, so it was no surprise that after five months the city of Jerusalem was handed over to him, taking advantage of the war difficulties of al-Kamil. The fact that this was regarded in the Arab as in the Christian world as <a href="/wiki/High_treason" title="High treason">high treason</a> did not matter to him. When certain members of the Knights Templar wrote al-Kamil a letter and offered to destroy Frederick if he lent them aid, al-Kamil handed the letter over to Frederick. Because the Patriarch of Jerusalem refused to crown him king, he set the crown on his own head.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_science">Literature and science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Literature and science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:De_Arte_Venandi_com_Avibus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/De_Arte_Venandi_com_Avibus.jpg/250px-De_Arte_Venandi_com_Avibus.jpg" width="250" height="158" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/De_Arte_Venandi_com_Avibus.jpg/375px-De_Arte_Venandi_com_Avibus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/De_Arte_Venandi_com_Avibus.jpg/500px-De_Arte_Venandi_com_Avibus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="964" data-file-height="611" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">An image from an old copy of <i><a href="/wiki/De_arte_venandi_cum_avibus" title="De arte venandi cum avibus">De arte venandi cum avibus</a></i></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Matthew_Paris_Elephant_from_Parker_MS_16_fol_151v.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Matthew_Paris_Elephant_from_Parker_MS_16_fol_151v.jpg/250px-Matthew_Paris_Elephant_from_Parker_MS_16_fol_151v.jpg" width="250" height="144" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Matthew_Paris_Elephant_from_Parker_MS_16_fol_151v.jpg/375px-Matthew_Paris_Elephant_from_Parker_MS_16_fol_151v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Matthew_Paris_Elephant_from_Parker_MS_16_fol_151v.jpg/500px-Matthew_Paris_Elephant_from_Parker_MS_16_fol_151v.jpg 2x" data-file-width="927" data-file-height="534" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Cremona elephant as depicted in the <i><a href="/wiki/Chronica_maiora" class="mw-redirect" title="Chronica maiora">Chronica maiora</a></i>, Part II, Parker Library, MS 16, fol. 151v - On parade during the visit of Frederick's brother-in-law <a href="/wiki/Richard,_1st_Earl_of_Cornwall" title="Richard, 1st Earl of Cornwall">Richard of Cornwall</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cremona" title="Cremona">Cremona</a> in 1241</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Besides his great tolerance (which, however, did not apply to Christian <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretics</a>), Frederick had a great thirst for knowledge and learning. Frederick employed <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Sicily" title="History of the Jews in Sicily">Jews from Sicily</a>, who had immigrated there from the holy land, at his court to translate Greek and Arabic works.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>He played a major role in promoting literature through the <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_School" title="Sicilian School">Sicilian School</a> of <a href="/wiki/Poetry" title="Poetry">poetry</a>. His Sicilian royal court in Palermo, saw the first use of a literary form of an <a href="/wiki/Italo-Romance" class="mw-redirect" title="Italo-Romance">Italo-Romance</a> language, <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_language" title="Sicilian language">Sicilian</a>. The poetry that emanated from the school had a significant influence on literature and on what was to become the modern <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian language</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The school and its poetry were saluted by <a href="/wiki/Dante" class="mw-redirect" title="Dante">Dante</a> and his peers and predate by at least a century the use of the Tuscan idiom as the elite literary language of Italy.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frederick II is the author of the first treatise on the subject of <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a>, <a href="/wiki/De_arte_venandi_cum_avibus" title="De arte venandi cum avibus"><i>De Arte Venandi cum Avibus</i></a> ("The Art of Hunting with Birds"). In the words of the historian <a href="/wiki/Charles_Homer_Haskins" title="Charles Homer Haskins">Charles Homer Haskins</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is a scientific book, approaching the subject from <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> but based closely on observation and experiment throughout, <i>Divisivus et Inquisitivus</i>, in the words of the preface; it is at the same time a <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholastic</a> book, minute and almost mechanical in its divisions and subdivisions. It is also a rigidly practical book, written by a falconer for falconers and condensing a long experience into systematic form for the use of others.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Frederick's pride in his mastery of the art is illustrated by the story that, when he was ordered to become a subject of the Great Khan (<a href="/wiki/Batu_Khan" title="Batu Khan">Batu</a>) and receive an office at the Khan's court, he remarked that he would make a good falconer, for he understood birds very well.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> He maintained up to fifty falconers at a time in his court, and in his letters he requested Arctic <a href="/wiki/Gyrfalcon" title="Gyrfalcon">gyrfalcons</a> from <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a> and even from <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>. One of the two existing versions was modified by his son <a href="/wiki/Manfred_of_Sicily" class="mw-redirect" title="Manfred of Sicily">Manfred</a>, also a keen falconer.</p>
<p>Frederick loved exotic animals in general: his <a href="/wiki/Menagerie" title="Menagerie">menagerie</a>, with which he impressed the cold cities of Northern Italy and Europe, included hounds, giraffes, cheetahs, lynxes, leopards, exotic birds and an elephant.<sup id="cite_ref-Cattaneo_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cattaneo-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was also alleged to have carried out a number of experiments on people.
