These experiments were recorded by the monk <a href="/wiki/Salimbene_di_Adam" title="Salimbene di Adam">Salimbene di Adam</a> in his <i>Chronicles</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Among the experiments were shutting a prisoner up in a cask to see if the soul could be observed escaping though a hole in the cask when the prisoner died; feeding two prisoners, having sent one out to hunt and the other to bed and then having them disemboweled to see which had digested his meal better; imprisoning children and then denying them any human contact to see if they would develop a natural language.</p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Language_deprivation_experiments" title="Language deprivation experiments">language deprivation experiment</a> young infants were <a href="/wiki/Feral_child" title="Feral child">raised without human interaction</a> in an attempt to determine if there was a <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural language</a> that they might demonstrate once their voices matured. It is claimed he was seeking to discover <a href="/wiki/Adamic_language" title="Adamic language">what language would have been imparted unto Adam and Eve</a> by God. In his <i>Chronicles</i> Salimbene wrote that Frederick bade "foster-mothers and nurses to suckle and bathe and wash the children, but in no ways to prattle or speak with them; for he would have learnt whether they would speak the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew language</a> (which had been the first), or <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>, or perchance the tongue of their parents of whom they had been born. But he laboured in vain, for the children could not live without clappings of the hands, and gestures, and gladness of countenance, and blandishments."</p>
<p>Frederick was also interested in the stars, and his court was host to many astrologers and astronomers, including <a href="/wiki/Michael_Scot" title="Michael Scot">Michael Scot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guido_Bonatti" title="Guido Bonatti">Guido Bonatti</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> He often sent letters to the leading scholars of the time (not only in Europe) asking for solutions to questions of science, mathematics and physics.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1224 he founded the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Naples" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Naples">University of Naples</a>, the world's oldest state university: now called <a href="/wiki/Universit%C3%A0_Federico_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Università Federico II">Università Federico II</a>, it remained the sole atheneum of Southern Italy for centuries.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Appearance">Appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:158px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mummified_corpse_of_frederick_II_hohenstaufen_in_palermo_(1781).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Mummified_corpse_of_frederick_II_hohenstaufen_in_palermo_%281781%29.jpg" width="156" height="513" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="156" data-file-height="513" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mummified_corpse_of_frederick_II_hohenstaufen_in_palermo_(1781).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1781 picture showing the mummified corpse of Frederick II in Palermo.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A Damascene chronicler, <a href="/wiki/Sibt_ibn_al-Jawzi" title="Sibt ibn al-Jawzi">Sibt ibn al-Jawzi</a>, left a physical description of Frederick based on the testimony of those who had seen the emperor in person in Jerusalem: "The Emperor was covered with red hair, was bald and myopic. Had he been a slave, he would not have fetched 200 <a href="/wiki/Dirhams" class="mw-redirect" title="Dirhams">dirhams</a> at market." Frederick's eyes were described variously as blue, or "green like those of a serpent."<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_reforms">Law reforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Law reforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>His 1231 <a href="/w/index.php?title=Edict_of_Salerno&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Edict of Salerno (page does not exist)">Edict of Salerno</a> (sometimes called "Constitution of Salerno") made the first legally fixed separation of the occupations of physician and apothecary. Physicians were forbidden to double as pharmacists and the prices of various medicinal remedies were fixed. This became a model for regulation of the practice of pharmacy throughout Europe. <sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was not able to extend his legal reforms beyond Sicily to the Empire. In 1232, he was forced by the German princes to promulgate the <i><a href="/wiki/Statutum_in_favorem_principum" title="Statutum in favorem principum">Statutum in favorem principum</a></i> ("statute in favor of princes"). It was a charter of liberties for the leading German princes at the expense of the lesser nobility and the entirety of the commoners. The princes gained whole power of jurisdiction, and the power to strike their own coins. The emperor lost his right to establish new cities, castles and mints over their territories. The <i>Statutum</i> severely weakened central authority in Germany. From 1232 the vassals of the emperor had a veto over imperial legislative decisions. Every new law established by the emperor had to be approved by the princes.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evaluation">Evaluation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Evaluation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Subventio_generalis" title="Subventio generalis">Subventio generalis</a></div>
<p>Historians rate Frederick II as a highly significant European monarch of the Middle Ages. This reputation was present even in Frederick's era. Lansing and English, two British historians, argue that medieval Palermo has been overlooked in favor of Paris and London:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>One effect of this approach has been to privilege historical winners, [and] aspects of medieval Europe that became important in later centuries, above all the nation state.... Arguably the liveliest cultural innovation in the 13th century was Mediterranean, centered on Frederick II's polyglot court and administration in Palermo.... Sicily and the Italian South in later centuries suffered a long slide into overtaxed poverty and marginality. Textbook narratives therefore focus not on medieval Palermo, with its Muslim and Jewish bureaucracies and Arabic-speaking monarch, but on the historical winners, Paris and London.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Modern medievalists no longer accept the notion, sponsored by the popes, of Frederick as an anti-Christian. They argue that Frederick understood himself as a Christian monarch in the sense of a <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine emperor">Byzantine emperor</a>, thus as God's "<a href="/wiki/Viceroy" title="Viceroy">viceroy</a>" on earth.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> Whatever his personal feelings toward religion, certainly submission to the pope did not enter into the matter in the slightest. This was in line with the Hohenstaufen <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kaiser-Idee&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kaiser-Idee (page does not exist)">Kaiser-Idee</a></i>, the ideology claiming the Holy Roman Emperor to be the legitimate successor to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Emperors</a>.</p>
<p>20th century treatments of Frederick vary from the sober (<a href="/w/index.php?title=Wolfgang_St%C3%BCrner&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Wolfgang Stürner (page does not exist)">Wolfgang Stürner</a>) to the dramatic (<a href="/wiki/Ernst_Kantorowicz" title="Ernst Kantorowicz">Ernst Kantorowicz</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> However, all agree on Frederick II's significance as Holy Roman Emperor.<sup id="cite_ref-FedericoTrecc_8-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FedericoTrecc-8">[8]</a></sup> In the judgment of British historian Geoffrey Barraclough, Frederick's extensive concessions to German princes—which he made in the hopes of securing his base for his Italian projects—undid the political power of his predecessors and postponed German unity for centuries.</p>
<p>However, the modern approach to Frederick II tends to be focused on the continuity between Frederick and his predecessors as Kings of Sicily and Holy Roman Emperors, and the similarities between him and other thirteenth-century monarchs. David Abulafia, in a biography subtitled "A Medieval Emperor," argues that Frederick’s reputation as an enlightened figure ahead of his time is undeserved, and that Frederick was mostly a conventionally Christian monarch who sought to rule in a conventional medieval manner.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Frederick left numerous children, legitimate and illegitimate:</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legitimate_issue">Legitimate issue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Legitimate issue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>First wife: <a href="/wiki/Constance_of_Aragon,_Holy_Roman_Empress" class="mw-redirect" title="Constance of Aragon, Holy Roman Empress">Constance of Aragon</a> (b. 1179 – d. 23 June 1222).<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup> Marriage: 15 August 1209, at <a href="/wiki/Messina" title="Messina">Messina</a>, Sicily.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Henry_(VII)_of_Germany" title="Henry (VII) of Germany">Henry (VII)</a> (1211 – 12 February 1242).<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Second wife: <a href="/wiki/Yolande_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Yolande of Jerusalem">Yolande of Jerusalem</a> (1212 – 25 April 1228).<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup> Marriage: 9 November 1225, at <a href="/wiki/Brindisi" title="Brindisi">Brindisi</a>, Apulia.
<ul>
<li>Margareta (November 1226 – August 1227).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">Conrad IV</a> (25 April 1228 – 21 May 1254).<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Third wife: <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_England" title="Isabella of England">Isabella of England</a> (1214 – 1 December 1241).<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup> Marriage: 15 July 1235, at Worms, Germany.
<ul>
<li>Jordan (born during the Spring of 1236, failed to survive the year);<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> this child was given the baptismal name Jordanus as he was baptized with water brought for that purpose from the Jordan river.</li>
<li>Agnes (b and d. 1237).</li>
<li>Henry Otto (18 February 1238&#160;– May 1253), named after <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III of England</a>, his uncle; appointed Governor of Sicily and promised to become <a href="/wiki/List_of_kings_of_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="List of kings of Jerusalem">King of Jerusalem</a> after his father died, but he, too, died within three years and was never crowned. Betrothed to many of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">Pope Innocent IV</a>'s nieces, but never married to any.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Margaret_of_Sicily" title="Margaret of Sicily">Margaret</a> (1 December 1241 – 8 August 1270), married <a href="/wiki/Albert_II,_Margrave_of_Meissen" title="Albert II, Margrave of Meissen">Albert, Landgrave of Thuringia</a>, later Margrave of Meissen.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Frederick had a relationship with <a href="/wiki/Bianca_Lancia" title="Bianca Lancia">Bianca Lancia</a> (ca. 1200/10-1230/46),<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup> possibly starting around 1225. One source states that it lasted 20 years. She bore him three children:
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Constance_II_of_Hohenstaufen" class="mw-redirect" title="Constance II of Hohenstaufen">Constance (Anna)</a> (1230 – April 1307), married <a href="/wiki/John_III_Ducas_Vatatzes" class="mw-redirect" title="John III Ducas Vatatzes">John III Ducas Vatatzes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Manfred_of_Sicily" class="mw-redirect" title="Manfred of Sicily">Manfred</a> (1232 – killed in battle, Benevento, 26 February 1266),<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup> first Regent, later King of Sicily.</li>
<li>Violante (1233–1264), married Riccardo Sanseverino, Conte di Caserta.</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Matthew of Paris relates the story of a marriage <i>in articulo mortis</i> (on her deathbed) between them when Bianca was dying,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> but this marriage was never recognized by the Church. Nevertheless, Bianca's children were apparently regarded by Frederick as legitimate, evidenced by his daughter Constance's marriage to the Nicaen Emperor, and his own will, in which he appointed Manfred as Prince of Taranto and Regent of Sicily.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mistresses_and_illegitimate_issue">Mistresses and illegitimate issue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mistresses and illegitimate issue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>Unknown name, Sicilian Countess. According to <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://fmg.ac/Projects/MedLands/GERMANY,%20Kings.htm#FriedrichIIGermanydied1250B">Medlands</a>, she was the first known mistress of Frederick II, then only King of Sicily. Her exact parentage is unknown, but the <i>Thomas Tusci Gesta Imperatorum et Pontificum</i> stated she was a <i>nobili comitissa quo in regno Sicilie erat heres</i>.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frederick_of_Pettorano" title="Frederick of Pettorano">Frederick of Pettorano</a> (1212/13 – aft. 1240), who fled to Spain with his wife and children in 1240.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Adelheid_of_Urslingen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Adelheid of Urslingen (page does not exist)">Adelheid (Adelaide) of Urslingen</a> (c. 1184 – c. 1222).<sup id="cite_ref-treccani_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treccani-42">[42]</a></sup> Her relationship with Frederick II took place during the time he stayed in Germany between 1215 and 1220. According to some sources,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> she was related to the Hohenburg family under the name <i>Alayta of Vohburg</i> (it: Alayta di Marano); but the most accepted theory stated she was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Conrad_of_Urslingen" class="mw-redirect" title="Conrad of Urslingen">Conrad of Urslingen</a>, Count of Assisi and Duke of Spoleto.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Enzo_of_Sardinia" title="Enzo of Sardinia">Enzo of Sardinia</a> (1215 –1272).<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>Caterina da Marano (1216/18 – aft. 1272), who married firstly with NN and secondly with Giacomo del Carretto, marquis of Noli and <a href="/wiki/Marquisate_of_Finale" title="Marquisate of Finale">Finale</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Matilda or Maria, from Antioch.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frederick_of_Antioch" title="Frederick of Antioch">Frederick of Antioch</a> (1221 – 1256).<sup id="cite_ref-Runciman26_38-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Runciman26-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>An unknown member of the Lancia family:<sup id="cite_ref-treccani_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treccani-42">[42]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Selvaggia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Selvaggia (page does not exist)">Selvaggia</a> (1221/23 – 1244), married <a href="/wiki/Ezzelino_III_da_Romano" title="Ezzelino III da Romano">Ezzelino III da Romano</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Manna, niece of Berardo di Castagna, Archbishop of Palermo:<sup id="cite_ref-treccani_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treccani-42">[42]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Richard_of_Chieti&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Richard of Chieti (page does not exist)">Richard of Chieti</a> (1224/25 – 26 May 1249).</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Anais of Brienne (c. 1205 – 1236), cousin of Isabella II of Jerusalem:<sup id="cite_ref-treccani_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treccani-42">[42]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li>Blanchefleur (1226 – 20 June 1279), Dominican nun in Montargis, France.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Richina of Wolfsöden (c. 1205 – 1236):<sup id="cite_ref-treccani_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-treccani-42">[42]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Margaret_of_Swabia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Margaret of Swabia (page does not exist)">Margaret of Swabia</a> (1230–1298), married Thomas of Aquino, count of Acerra.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Unknown mistress:
<ul>
<li>Gerhard (died after 1255).</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancestry">Ancestry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ancestry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Pabst, Bernhard (2002). <i>Gregor von Montesacro und die geistige Kultur Süditaliens unter Friedrich II. Franz Steiner Verlag. p.&#160;307. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/3-515-07909-2" title="Special:BookSources/3-515-07909-2">3-515-07909-2</a>. <q>Vor allem die Astrologie gewann immer an Einfluß und bestimmte teilweise sogar das Handeln der politischen Entscheidungsträger – die Gestalt des Hofastrologen Michael Scotus... ist ein nur ein prominenter Beleg (lit.: Mainly astrology gained ever more influence and in parts it even decided the acting of the political decision makers – the figure of court astrologer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Scot" title="Michael Scot">Michael Scot</a> is just one prominent reference [among others].)</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFrederick+II%2C+Holy+Roman+Emperor&amp;rft.aufirst=Bernhard&amp;rft.aulast=Pabst&amp;rft.btitle=Gregor+von+Montesacro+und+die+geistige+Kultur+S%C3%BCditaliens+unter+Friedrich+II.+%28Montesacro-Forschungen%29&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=3-515-07909-2&amp;rft.pages=307&amp;rft.pub=Franz+Steiner+Verlag&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

A History Of Western Astrology. <b>II</b>. <a href="/wiki/Continuum_Books" class="mw-redirect" title="Continuum Books">Continuum Books</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4411-8129-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4411-8129-9">978-1-4411-8129-9</a>. <q>Bonatti, for instance, was perhaps the most famous astrologer of his day and apparently advised Frederick II on military matters.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFrederick+II%2C+Holy+Roman+Emperor&amp;rft.aufirst=Nicholas&amp;rft.aulast=Campion&amp;rft.btitle=The+Medieval+And+Modern+Worlds&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4411-8129-9&amp;rft.pub=Continuum+Books&amp;rft.series=A+History+Of+Western+Astrology&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.skyscript.co.uk%2Frev_c2.html&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation magazine">Görich, Knut. "Stupor mundi – Staunen der Welt". <i><a href="/wiki/Damals" title="Damals">Damals</a></i> (in German). Vol.&#160;42 no.&#160;10/2010. p.&#160;61. <q>Da die Demonstration gelehrten Wissens an den arabischen Höfen besonderen Stellenwert hatte, waren die Fragen, die Friedrich an muslimische Gelehrte schickte – sie betrafen optische Phänomene wie die Krümmung eines Gegenstandes im Wasser ebenso wie die angebliche Unsterblichkeit der Seele —, nicht nur Ausdruck der persönlichen Wissbegier des Kaisers (lit.:Because demonstration of scholarly knowledge played an important role at the Arab courts, the questions Frederick sent to Muslim scholars, regarding optical phenomena like the <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">curving of objects in water</a> as well as the alleged immortality of the soul, were not merely a sign of the emperor's personal intellectual curiosity).</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFrederick+II%2C+Holy+Roman+Emperor&amp;rft.aufirst=Knut&amp;rft.aulast=G%C3%B6rich&amp;rft.btitle=Stupor+mundi+%E2%80%93+Staunen+der+Welt&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pages=61&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sibt ibn al-Jawzi, <i>Mirat al-Zaman', cited in Malouf, Amin</i> The Crusades Through Arab Eyes <i>(J. Rothschild trans.) Saqi Books, 2006, p.230</i></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Rashdall, Hastings (1895). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.ca/books?id=7YYRAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA84"><i>The Universities of Europe in the Middle Ages</i></a>. Clarendon Press. p.&#160;85<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 November</span> 2016</span>. <q>The physician [...] was not allowed to sell his own drugs ('nec ipse etiam habebit propriam stationem').</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFrederick+II%2C+Holy+Roman+Emperor&amp;rft.aufirst=Hastings&amp;rft.aulast=Rashdall&amp;rft.btitle=The+Universities+of+Europe+in+the+Middle+Ages&amp;rft.date=1895&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=85&amp;rft.pub=Clarendon+Press&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.ca%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D7YYRAAAAYAAJ%26pg%3DPA84&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Carol Lansing and Edward D. English, eds. (2012). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Re-1YpI9ObsC&amp;pg=PA1964"><i>A Companion to the Medieval World</i></a>. <li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Abulafia, David (1988). <i>Frederick II. A Medieval Emperor</i>. Penguin Press. p.&#160;436.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AFrederick+II%2C+Holy+Roman+Emperor&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft.aulast=Abulafia&amp;rft.btitle=Frederick+II.+A+Medieval+Emperor&amp;rft.date=1988&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=436&amp;rft.pub=Penguin+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Thomas Curtis Van Cleve's <i>The Emperor Frederick II of Hohenstaufen: Immutator Mundi</i> (Oxford, 1972). Page 381: "Certainly there is some evidence that a son, Jordanus, was born in the year 1236, and died shortly afterwards, but the only son of Frederick II and Isabella of England whose birth can be firmly established was a second Henry, born in 1238, and named after his uncle, Henry III, the King of England."</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ufYKAAAAYAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=Chronica+Majora#PPA572,M1">"Monachi Sancti Albani, Chronica Majora</a>, Matthew of Paris, pg 572</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A charter issued by Emperor Frederick II dated 1248 was witnessed by Manfred [III], Marquis of Lancia, "our beloved kinsman" [dilectus affinis noster]. The word here used for kinsman is "affinis," that is, kinsman by marriage, not blood. A transcript of this charter is published in <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=C1cBAAAAYAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=Historia+diplomatica+Friderica+Secundi#PPA672,M1">Huillard-Bréholles, Historia diplomatica Friderica Secundi, 6(2) (1861): 670–672</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-treccani-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-treccani_42-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-treccani_42-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-treccani_42-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-treccani_42-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-treccani_42-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.treccani.it/enciclopedia/figli-federico-ii_(Federiciana)/">"Federico II, figli"</a>, <i>Enciclopedia Federiciana</i> (Istituto dell'Enciclopedia Italiana, 2005).</span></li>
